Both were charged with membership in the Nazi Party, at that time illegal for Reichswehr personnel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWheeler-Bennett1967218_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWheeler-Bennett1967218-144">[140]</a></sup> The prosecution argued that the Nazi Party was an extremist party, prompting defence lawyer Hans Frank to call on Hitler to testify.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWheeler-Bennett1967216_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWheeler-Bennett1967216-145">[141]</a></sup> On 25 September 1930, Hitler testified that his party would pursue political power solely through democratic elections,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWheeler-Bennett1967218–219_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWheeler-Bennett1967218–219-146">[142]</a></sup> which won him many supporters in the officer corps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWheeler-Bennett1967222_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWheeler-Bennett1967222-147">[143]</a></sup>
</p><p>Brüning's austerity measures brought little economic improvement and were extremely unpopular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalperin1965449_''et._seq''_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalperin1965449_''et._seq''-148">[144]</a></sup> Hitler exploited this by targeting his political messages specifically at people who had been affected by the inflation of the 1920s and the Depression, such as farmers, war veterans, and the middle class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalperin1965434–436,_471_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalperin1965434–436,_471-149">[145]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although Hitler had terminated his Austrian citizenship in 1925, he did not acquire German citizenship for almost seven years. This meant that he was <a href="/wiki/Statelessness" title="Statelessness">stateless</a>, legally unable to run for public office, and still faced the risk of deportation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShirer1960130_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShirer1960130-150">[146]</a></sup> On 25 February 1932, the interior minister of <a href="/wiki/Free_State_of_Brunswick" title="Free State of Brunswick">Brunswick</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dietrich_Klagges" title="Dietrich Klagges">Dietrich Klagges</a>, who was a member of the Nazi Party, appointed Hitler as administrator for the state's delegation to the <a href="/wiki/Reichsrat_(Germany)" title="Reichsrat (Germany)">Reichsrat</a> in Berlin, making Hitler a citizen of Brunswick,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHinrichs2007_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHinrichs2007-151">[147]</a></sup> and thus of Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalperin1965476_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalperin1965476-152">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hitler ran against Hindenburg in the <a href="/wiki/1932_German_presidential_election" title="1932 German presidential election">1932 presidential elections</a>. A speech to the Industry Club in <a href="/wiki/D%C3%BCsseldorf" title="Düsseldorf">Düsseldorf</a> on 27 January 1932 won him support from many of Germany's most powerful industrialists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalperin1965468–471_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalperin1965468–471-153">[149]</a></sup> Hindenburg had support from various nationalist, monarchist, Catholic, and <a href="/wiki/Republicanism" title="Republicanism">republican</a> parties, and some <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_Party_of_Germany" title="Social Democratic Party of Germany">Social Democrats</a>. Hitler used the campaign slogan "<span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Hitler über Deutschland</i></span>" ("Hitler over Germany"), a reference to his political ambitions and his campaigning by aircraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBullock1962201_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBullock1962201-154">[150]</a></sup> He was one of the first politicians to use aircraft travel for political purposes, and used it effectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoffman1989_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoffman1989-155">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008227_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008227-156">[152]</a></sup> Hitler came in second in both rounds of the election, garnering more than 35 per cent of the vote in the final election. Although he lost to Hindenburg, this election established Hitler as a strong force in German politics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalperin1965477–479_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalperin1965477–479-157">[153]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Appointment_as_chancellor">Appointment as chancellor</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1972-026-11,_Macht%C3%BCbernahme_Hitlers.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="542" data-file-width="778" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1972-026-11%2C_Macht%C3%BCbernahme_Hitlers.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1972-026-11%2C_Macht%C3%BCbernahme_Hitlers.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1972-026-11%2C_Macht%C3%BCbernahme_Hitlers.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1972-026-11%2C_Macht%C3%BCbernahme_Hitlers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1972-026-11%2C_Macht%C3%BCbernahme_Hitlers.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1972-026-11%2C_Macht%C3%BCbernahme_Hitlers.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1972-026-11,_Macht%C3%BCbernahme_Hitlers.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hitler, at the window of the <a href="/wiki/Reich_Chancellery" title="Reich Chancellery">Reich Chancellery</a>, receives an ovation on the evening of his inauguration as <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_Germany" title="Chancellor of Germany">chancellor</a>, 30 January 1933.</div></div></div>
<p>The absence of an effective government prompted two influential politicians, <a href="/wiki/Franz_von_Papen" title="Franz von Papen">Franz von Papen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Hugenberg" title="Alfred Hugenberg">Alfred Hugenberg</a>, along with several other industrialists and businessmen, to write a letter to Hindenburg. The signers urged Hindenburg to appoint Hitler as leader of a government "independent from parliamentary parties", which could turn into a movement that would "enrapture millions of people".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELetter_to_Hindenburg,_1932_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELetter_to_Hindenburg,_1932-158">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFox_News,_2003_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFox_News,_2003-159">[155]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hindenburg reluctantly agreed to appoint Hitler as chancellor after two further parliamentary elections—in July and November 1932—had not resulted in the formation of a majority government. Hitler headed a short-lived coalition government formed by the Nazi Party (which had the most seats in the Reichstag) and Hugenberg's party, the <a href="/wiki/German_National_People%27s_Party" title="German National People's Party">German National People's Party</a> (DNVP). On 30 January 1933, the new cabinet was sworn in during a brief ceremony in Hindenburg's office. The Nazi Party gained three posts: Hitler was named chancellor, <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Frick" title="Wilhelm Frick">Wilhelm Frick</a> Minister of the Interior, and Hermann Göring Minister of the Interior for Prussia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShirer1960184_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShirer1960184-160">[156]</a></sup> Hitler had insisted on the ministerial positions as a way to gain control over the police in much of Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2003307_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2003307-161">[157]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reichstag_fire_and_March_elections">Reichstag fire and March elections</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Reichstag_fire" title="Reichstag fire">Reichstag fire</a></div>
<p>As chancellor, Hitler worked against attempts by the Nazi Party's opponents to build a majority government. Because of the political stalemate, he asked Hindenburg to again dissolve the Reichstag, and elections were scheduled for early March. On 27 February 1933, the <a href="/wiki/Reichstag_fire" title="Reichstag fire">Reichstag building was set on fire</a>. Göring blamed a communist plot, as Dutch communist <a href="/wiki/Marinus_van_der_Lubbe" title="Marinus van der Lubbe">Marinus van der Lubbe</a> was found in incriminating circumstances inside the burning building.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBullock1962262_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBullock1962262-162">[158]</a></sup> Until the 1960s, some historians including <a href="/wiki/William_L._Shirer" title="William L. Shirer">William L. Shirer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alan_Bullock" title="Alan Bullock">Alan Bullock</a> thought the Nazi Party itself was responsible;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShirer1960192_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShirer1960192-163">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBullock1999262_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBullock1999262-164">[160]</a></sup> the current consensus of nearly all historians is that van der Lubbe actually set the fire alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw1999456–458,_731–732_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw1999456–458,_731–732-165">[161]</a></sup> At Hitler's urging, Hindenburg responded by signing the <a href="/wiki/Reichstag_Fire_Decree" title="Reichstag Fire Decree">Reichstag Fire Decree</a> of 28 February, drafted by the Nazis, which suspended basic rights and allowed detention without trial. The decree was permitted under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Article_48" title="Article 48">Article 48</a> of the Weimar Constitution, which gave the president the power to take emergency measures to protect public safety and order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShirer1960194,_274_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShirer1960194,_274-166">[162]</a></sup> Activities of the <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_Germany" title="Communist Party of Germany">German Communist Party</a> (KPD) were suppressed, and some 4,000 KPD members were arrested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShirer1960194_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShirer1960194-167">[163]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to political campaigning, the Nazi Party engaged in paramilitary violence and the spread of anti-communist propaganda in the days preceding <a href="/wiki/March_1933_German_federal_election" title="March 1933 German federal election">the election</a>. On election day, 6 March 1933, the Nazi Party's share of the vote increased to 43.9 per cent, and the party acquired the largest number of seats in parliament. Hitler's party failed to secure an absolute majority, necessitating another coalition with the DNVP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBullock1962265_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBullock1962265-168">[164]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Day_of_Potsdam_and_the_Enabling_Act">Day of Potsdam and the Enabling Act</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Enabling_Act_of_1933" title="Enabling Act of 1933">Enabling Act of 1933</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-S38324,_Tag_von_Potsdam,_Adolf_Hitler,_Paul_v._Hindenburg.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1400" data-file-width="1920" decoding="async" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-S38324%2C_Tag_von_Potsdam%2C_Adolf_Hitler%2C_Paul_v._Hindenburg.jpg/260px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-S38324%2C_Tag_von_Potsdam%2C_Adolf_Hitler%2C_Paul_v._Hindenburg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-S38324%2C_Tag_von_Potsdam%2C_Adolf_Hitler%2C_Paul_v._Hindenburg.jpg/390px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-S38324%2C_Tag_von_Potsdam%2C_Adolf_Hitler%2C_Paul_v._Hindenburg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-S38324%2C_Tag_von_Potsdam%2C_Adolf_Hitler%2C_Paul_v._Hindenburg.jpg/520px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-S38324%2C_Tag_von_Potsdam%2C_Adolf_Hitler%2C_Paul_v._Hindenburg.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-S38324,_Tag_von_Potsdam,_Adolf_Hitler,_Paul_v._Hindenburg.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hitler and <a href="/wiki/Paul_von_Hindenburg" title="Paul von Hindenburg">Paul von Hindenburg</a> on the Day of Potsdam, 21 March 1933</div></div></div>
<p>On 21 March 1933, the new Reichstag was constituted with an opening ceremony at the <a href="/wiki/Garrison_Church_(Potsdam)" title="Garrison Church (Potsdam)">Garrison Church</a> in <a href="/wiki/Potsdam" title="Potsdam">Potsdam</a>. This "Day of Potsdam" was held to demonstrate unity between the Nazi movement and the old <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussian</a> elite and military. Hitler appeared in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Morning_coat" title="Morning coat">morning coat</a> and humbly greeted Hindenburg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECity_of_Potsdam_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECity_of_Potsdam-169">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShirer1960196–197_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShirer1960196–197-170">[166]</a></sup>
</p><p>To achieve full political control despite not having an absolute majority in parliament, Hitler's government brought the <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Ermächtigungsgesetz</i></span> (Enabling Act) to a vote in the newly elected Reichstag. The Act – officially titled the <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Gesetz zur Behebung der Not von Volk und Reich</i></span> ("Law to Remedy the Distress of People and Reich") – gave Hitler's cabinet the power to enact laws without the consent of the Reichstag for four years. These laws could (with certain exceptions) deviate from the constitution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShirer1960198_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShirer1960198-171">[167]</a></sup> Since it would affect the constitution, the Enabling Act required a two-thirds majority to pass. Leaving nothing to chance, the Nazis used the provisions of the Reichstag Fire Decree to arrest all 81 Communist deputies (in spite of their virulent campaign against the party, the Nazis had allowed the KPD to contest the election)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2003335_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2003335-172">[168]</a></sup> and prevent several Social Democrats from attending.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShirer1960196_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShirer1960196-173">[169]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 23 March 1933, the Reichstag assembled at the <a href="/wiki/Kroll_Opera_House" title="Kroll Opera House">Kroll Opera House</a> under turbulent circumstances. Ranks of SA men served as guards inside the building, while large groups outside opposing the proposed legislation shouted slogans and threats towards the arriving members of parliament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBullock1999269_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBullock1999269-174">[170]</a></sup> After Hitler verbally promised Centre party leader <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Kaas" title="Ludwig Kaas">Ludwig Kaas</a> that Hindenburg would retain his power of veto, Kaas announced the Centre Party would support the Enabling Act. The Act passed by a vote of 444–94, with all parties except the Social Democrats voting in favour. The Enabling Act, along with the Reichstag Fire Decree, transformed Hitler's government into a de facto legal dictatorship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShirer1960199_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShirer1960199-175">[171]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dictatorship">Dictatorship</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>At the risk of appearing to talk nonsense I tell you that the National Socialist movement will go on for 1,000 years! ... Don't forget how people laughed at me 15 years ago when I declared that one day I would govern Germany. They laugh now, just as foolishly, when I declare that I shall remain in power!<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE''Time'',_1934_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE''Time'',_1934-176">[172]</a></sup></p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Adolf Hitler to a British correspondent in Berlin, June 1934</cite></div></blockquote>
<p>Having achieved full control over the legislative and executive branches of government, Hitler and his allies began to suppress the remaining opposition. The Social Democratic Party was banned and its assets seized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShirer1960201_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShirer1960201-177">[173]</a></sup> While many trade union delegates were in Berlin for May Day activities, SA stormtroopers occupied union offices around the country. On 2 May 1933, all trade unions were forced to dissolve and their leaders were arrested. Some were sent to <a href="/wiki/Nazi_concentration_camps" title="Nazi concentration camps">concentration camps</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShirer1960202_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShirer1960202-178">[174]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/German_Labour_Front" title="German Labour Front">German Labour Front</a> was formed as an umbrella organisation to represent all workers, administrators, and company owners, thus reflecting the concept of Nazism in the spirit of Hitler's <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Volksgemeinschaft" title="Volksgemeinschaft">Volksgemeinschaft</a></i></span> ("people's community").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2003350–374_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2003350–374-179">[175]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1990-048-29A,_Adolf_Hitler_retouched.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="514" data-file-width="785" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1990-048-29A%2C_Adolf_Hitler_retouched.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1990-048-29A%2C_Adolf_Hitler_retouched.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1990-048-29A%2C_Adolf_Hitler_retouched.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1990-048-29A%2C_Adolf_Hitler_retouched.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1990-048-29A%2C_Adolf_Hitler_retouched.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1990-048-29A%2C_Adolf_Hitler_retouched.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1990-048-29A,_Adolf_Hitler_retouched.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In 1934, Hitler became Germany's head of state with the title of <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a href="/wiki/F%C3%BChrer" title="Führer">Führer und Reichskanzler</a></i></span> (leader and chancellor of the Reich).</div></div></div>
<p>By the end of June, the other parties had been intimidated into disbanding. This included the Nazis' nominal coalition partner, the DNVP; with the SA's help, Hitler forced its leader, Hugenberg, to resign on 29 June. On 14 July 1933, the Nazi Party was declared the only legal political party in Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2003350–374_179-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2003350–374-179">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShirer1960201_177-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShirer1960201-177">[173]</a></sup> The demands of the SA for more political and military power caused anxiety among military, industrial, and political leaders. In response, Hitler purged the entire SA leadership in the <a href="/wiki/Night_of_the_Long_Knives" title="Night of the Long Knives">Night of the Long Knives</a>, which took place from 30 June to 2 July 1934.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008309–314_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008309–314-180">[176]</a></sup> Hitler targeted Ernst Röhm and other SA leaders who, along with a number of Hitler's political adversaries (such as Gregor Strasser and former chancellor <a href="/wiki/Kurt_von_Schleicher" title="Kurt von Schleicher">Kurt von Schleicher</a>), were rounded up, arrested, and shot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETames20084–5_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETames20084–5-181">[177]</a></sup> While the international community and some Germans were shocked by the murders, many in Germany believed Hitler was restoring order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008313–315_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008313–315-182">[178]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 2 August 1934, Hindenburg died. The previous day, the cabinet had enacted the "Law Concerning the Highest State Office of the Reich".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery200563_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery200563-4">[3]</a></sup> This law stated that upon Hindenburg's death, the office of president would be abolished and its powers merged with those of the chancellor. Hitler thus became head of state as well as head of government, and was formally named as <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a href="/wiki/F%C3%BChrer" title="Führer">Führer und Reichskanzler</a></i></span> (leader and chancellor),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShirer1960226–227_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShirer1960226–227-3">[2]</a></sup> although <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Reichskanzler</i></span> was eventually quietly dropped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans200544_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans200544-183">[179]</a></sup> With this action, Hitler eliminated the last legal remedy by which he could be removed from office.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShirer1960229_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShirer1960229-184">[180]</a></sup>
</p><p>As head of state, Hitler became commander-in-chief of the armed forces. Immediately after Hindenburg's death, at the instigation of the leadership of the <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Reichswehr</i></span>, the traditional loyalty oath of soldiers was altered to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hitler_oath" title="Hitler oath">affirm loyalty to Hitler personally, by name</a>, rather than to the office of commander-in-chief (which was later renamed to supreme commander) or the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBullock1962309_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBullock1962309-185">[181]</a></sup> On 19 August, the merger of the presidency with the chancellorship was approved by 88 per cent of the electorate voting in a <a href="/wiki/1934_German_referendum" title="1934 German referendum">plebiscite</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2005110_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2005110-186">[182]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Standarte_Adolf_Hitlers.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="670" data-file-width="670" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Standarte_Adolf_Hitlers.svg/170px-Standarte_Adolf_Hitlers.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Standarte_Adolf_Hitlers.svg/255px-Standarte_Adolf_Hitlers.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Standarte_Adolf_Hitlers.svg/340px-Standarte_Adolf_Hitlers.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Standarte_Adolf_Hitlers.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hitler's personal standard</div></div></div>
<p>In early 1938, Hitler used blackmail to consolidate his hold over the military by instigating the <a href="/wiki/Blomberg%E2%80%93Fritsch_affair" title="Blomberg–Fritsch affair">Blomberg–Fritsch affair</a>. Hitler forced his War Minister, Field Marshal <a href="/wiki/Werner_von_Blomberg" title="Werner von Blomberg">Werner von Blomberg</a>, to resign by using a police dossier that showed that Blomberg's new wife had a record for prostitution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008392,_393_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008392,_393-187">[183]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShirer1960312_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShirer1960312-188">[184]</a></sup> Army commander Colonel-General <a href="/wiki/Werner_von_Fritsch" title="Werner von Fritsch">Werner von Fritsch</a> was removed after the <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Schutzstaffel" title="Schutzstaffel">Schutzstaffel</a></i></span> (SS) produced allegations that he had engaged in a homosexual relationship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008393–397_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008393–397-189">[185]</a></sup> Both men had fallen into disfavour because they objected to Hitler's demand to make the <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Wehrmacht" title="Wehrmacht">Wehrmacht</a></i></span> ready for war as early as 1938.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShirer1960308_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShirer1960308-190">[186]</a></sup> Hitler assumed Blomberg's title of Commander-in-Chief, thus taking personal command of the armed forces. He replaced the Ministry of War with the <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Oberkommando_der_Wehrmacht" title="Oberkommando der Wehrmacht">Oberkommando der Wehrmacht</a></i></span> (OKW), headed by General <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Keitel" title="Wilhelm Keitel">Wilhelm Keitel</a>. On the same day, sixteen generals were stripped of their commands and 44 more were transferred; all were suspected of not being sufficiently pro-Nazi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShirer1960318–319_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShirer1960318–319-191">[187]</a></sup> By early February 1938, twelve more generals had been removed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008397–398_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008397–398-192">[188]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hitler took care to give his dictatorship the appearance of legality. Many of his decrees were explicitly based on the Reichstag Fire Decree and hence on Article 48 of the Weimar Constitution. The Reichstag renewed the Enabling Act twice, each time for a four-year period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShirer1960274_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShirer1960274-193">[189]</a></sup> While elections to the Reichstag were still held (in 1933, 1936, and 1938), voters were presented with a single list of Nazis and pro-Nazi "guests" which carried with well over 90 per cent of the vote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERead2004344_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERead2004344-194">[190]</a></sup> These elections were held in far-from-secret conditions; the Nazis threatened severe reprisals against anyone who did not vote or dared to vote no.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2005109–111_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2005109–111-195">[191]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nazi_Germany">Nazi Germany</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy_and_culture">Economy and culture</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Nazi_Germany" title="Economy of Nazi Germany">Economy of Nazi Germany</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-04062A,_N%C3%BCrnberg,_Reichsparteitag,_SA-_und_SS-Appell.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="795" data-file-width="553" decoding="async" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-04062A%2C_N%C3%BCrnberg%2C_Reichsparteitag%2C_SA-_und_SS-Appell.jpg/170px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-04062A%2C_N%C3%BCrnberg%2C_Reichsparteitag%2C_SA-_und_SS-Appell.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-04062A%2C_N%C3%BCrnberg%2C_Reichsparteitag%2C_SA-_und_SS-Appell.jpg/255px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-04062A%2C_N%C3%BCrnberg%2C_Reichsparteitag%2C_SA-_und_SS-Appell.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-04062A%2C_N%C3%BCrnberg%2C_Reichsparteitag%2C_SA-_und_SS-Appell.jpg/340px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-04062A%2C_N%C3%BCrnberg%2C_Reichsparteitag%2C_SA-_und_SS-Appell.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_102-04062A,_N%C3%BCrnberg,_Reichsparteitag,_SA-_und_SS-Appell.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ceremony honouring the dead (Totenehrung) on the terrace in front of the Hall of Honour (Ehrenhalle) at the <a href="/wiki/Nazi_party_rally_grounds" title="Nazi party rally grounds">Nazi party rally grounds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg" title="Nuremberg">Nuremberg</a>, September 1934</div></div></div>
<p>In August 1934, Hitler appointed <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Reichsbank</i></span> President <a href="/wiki/Hjalmar_Schacht" title="Hjalmar Schacht">Hjalmar Schacht</a> as Minister of Economics, and in the following year, as Plenipotentiary for War Economy in charge of preparing the economy for war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcNab200954_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcNab200954-196">[192]</a></sup> Reconstruction and rearmament were financed through <a href="/wiki/Mefo_bills" title="Mefo bills">Mefo bills</a>, printing money, and seizing the assets of people arrested as enemies of the State, including Jews.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShirer1960259–260_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShirer1960259–260-197">[193]</a></sup> Unemployment fell from six million in 1932 to one million in 1936.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShirer1960258_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShirer1960258-198">[194]</a></sup> Hitler oversaw one of the largest infrastructure improvement campaigns in German history, leading to the construction of dams, <a href="/wiki/Autobahn" title="Autobahn">autobahns</a>, railroads, and other civil works. Wages were slightly lower in the mid to late 1930s compared with wages during the Weimar Republic, while the cost of living increased by 25 per cent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShirer1960262_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShirer1960262-199">[195]</a></sup> The average work week increased during the shift to a war economy; by 1939, the average German was working between 47 and 50 hours a week.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcNab200954–57_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcNab200954–57-200">[196]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hitler's government sponsored <a href="/wiki/Nazi_architecture" title="Nazi architecture">architecture</a> on an immense scale. <a href="/wiki/Albert_Speer" title="Albert Speer">Albert Speer</a>, instrumental in implementing Hitler's classicist reinterpretation of German culture, was placed in charge of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Welthauptstadt_Germania" title="Welthauptstadt Germania">proposed architectural renovations of Berlin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpeer1971118–119_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpeer1971118–119-201">[197]</a></sup> Despite a threatened <a href="/wiki/1936_Summer_Olympics#Boycott_debate" title="1936 Summer Olympics">multi-nation boycott</a>, Germany hosted the 1936 Olympic Games. Hitler <a href="/wiki/List_of_people_who_have_opened_the_Olympic_Games" title="List of people who have opened the Olympic Games">officiated</a> at the opening ceremonies and attended events at both the <a href="/wiki/1936_Winter_Olympics" title="1936 Winter Olympics">Winter Games</a> in <a href="/wiki/Garmisch-Partenkirchen" title="Garmisch-Partenkirchen">Garmisch-Partenkirchen</a> and the <a href="/wiki/1936_Summer_Olympics" title="1936 Summer Olympics">Summer Games</a> in Berlin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2005570–572_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2005570–572-202">[198]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rearmament_and_new_alliances">Rearmament and new alliances</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Axis_powers" title="Axis powers">Axis powers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tripartite_Pact" title="Tripartite Pact">Tripartite Pact</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_re-armament" title="German re-armament">German re-armament</a></div>
<p>In a meeting with German military leaders on 3 February 1933, Hitler spoke of "conquest for <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Lebensraum</i></span> in the East and its ruthless Germanisation" as his ultimate foreign policy objectives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinberg197026–27_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinberg197026–27-203">[199]</a></sup> In March, Prince Bernhard Wilhelm von Bülow, secretary at the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Foreign_Office" title="Federal Foreign Office">Auswärtiges Amt</a> (Foreign Office), issued a statement of major foreign policy aims: <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Anschluss" title="Anschluss">Anschluss</a></i></span> with Austria, the restoration of Germany's national borders of 1914, rejection of military restrictions under the Treaty of Versailles, the return of the former German colonies in Africa, and a German zone of influence in Eastern Europe. Hitler found Bülow's goals to be too modest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw1999490–491_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw1999490–491-204">[200]</a></sup> In speeches during this period, he stressed the peaceful goals of his policies and a willingness to work within international agreements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw1999492,_555–556,_586–587_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw1999492,_555–556,_586–587-205">[201]</a></sup> At the first meeting of his cabinet in 1933, Hitler prioritised military spending over unemployment relief.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarr197223_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarr197223-206">[202]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hitlermusso2_edit.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1100" data-file-width="697" decoding="async" height="268" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b1/Hitlermusso2_edit.jpg/170px-Hitlermusso2_edit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b1/Hitlermusso2_edit.jpg/255px-Hitlermusso2_edit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b1/Hitlermusso2_edit.jpg/340px-Hitlermusso2_edit.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hitlermusso2_edit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Benito_Mussolini" title="Benito Mussolini">Benito Mussolini</a> with Hitler on 25 October 1936, when the axis between Italy and Germany was declared</div></div></div>
<p>Germany withdrew from the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Disarmament_Conference" title="World Disarmament Conference">World Disarmament Conference</a> in October 1933.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008297_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008297-207">[203]</a></sup> In January 1935, over 90 per cent of the people of the <a href="/wiki/Saarland" title="Saarland">Saarland</a>, then under League of Nations administration, <a href="/wiki/1935_Saar_status_referendum" title="1935 Saar status referendum">voted to unite with Germany</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShirer1960283_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShirer1960283-208">[204]</a></sup> That March, Hitler announced an expansion of the Wehrmacht to 600,000 members – six times the number permitted by the Versailles Treaty – including development of an air force (<span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Luftwaffe" title="Luftwaffe">Luftwaffe</a></i></span>) and an increase in the size of the navy (<span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Kriegsmarine" title="Kriegsmarine">Kriegsmarine</a></i></span>). Britain, France, Italy, and the League of Nations condemned these violations of the Treaty, but did nothing to stop it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMesserschmidt1990601–602_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMesserschmidt1990601–602-209">[205]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMartin2008_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMartin2008-210">[206]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Anglo-German_Naval_Agreement" title="Anglo-German Naval Agreement">Anglo-German Naval Agreement</a> (AGNA) of 18 June allowed German tonnage to increase to 35 per cent of that of the British navy. Hitler called the signing of the AGNA "the happiest day of his life", believing that the agreement marked the beginning of the Anglo-German alliance he had predicted in <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Mein Kampf</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHildebrand197339_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHildebrand197339-211">[207]</a></sup> France and Italy were not consulted before the signing, directly undermining the League of Nations and setting the Treaty of Versailles on the path towards irrelevance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoberts1975_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoberts1975-212">[208]</a></sup>
</p><p>Germany <a href="/wiki/Remilitarization_of_the_Rhineland" title="Remilitarization of the Rhineland">reoccupied</a> the demilitarised zone in the Rhineland in March 1936, in violation of the Versailles Treaty. Hitler also sent troops to Spain to support <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General_Franco" title="General Franco">General Franco</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Civil_War" title="Spanish Civil War">Spanish Civil War</a> after receiving an appeal for help in July 1936. At the same time, Hitler continued his efforts to create an Anglo-German alliance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMesserschmidt1990630–631_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMesserschmidt1990630–631-213">[209]</a></sup> In August 1936, in response to a growing economic crisis caused by his rearmament efforts, Hitler ordered Göring to implement a <a href="/wiki/Four_Year_Plan" title="Four Year Plan">Four Year Plan</a> to prepare Germany for war within the next four years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery,_''Origins_of_WWII_Reconsidered''1999_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery,_''Origins_of_WWII_Reconsidered''1999-214">[210]</a></sup> The plan envisaged an all-out struggle between "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Judeo-Bolshevism" title="Judeo-Bolshevism">Judeo-Bolshevism</a>" and German Nazism, which in Hitler's view required a committed effort of rearmament regardless of the economic costs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarr197256–57_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarr197256–57-215">[211]</a></sup>
</p><p>In October 1936, Count <a href="/wiki/Galeazzo_Ciano" title="Galeazzo Ciano">Galeazzo Ciano</a>, foreign minister of Mussolini's government, visited Germany, where he signed a <a href="/wiki/Italo-German_protocol_of_23_October_1936" title="Italo-German protocol of 23 October 1936">Nine-Point Protocol</a> as an expression of <i>rapprochement</i> and had a personal meeting with Hitler. On 1 November, Mussolini declared an "axis" between Germany and Italy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoeschel201869–70_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoeschel201869–70-216">[212]</a></sup> On 25 November, Germany signed the <a href="/wiki/Anti-Comintern_Pact" title="Anti-Comintern Pact">Anti-Comintern Pact</a> with <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">Japan</a>. Britain, China, Italy, and Poland were also invited to join the Anti-Comintern Pact, but only Italy signed in 1937. Hitler abandoned his plan of an Anglo-German alliance, blaming "inadequate" British leadership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMesserschmidt1990642_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMesserschmidt1990642-217">[213]</a></sup> At a meeting in the <a href="/wiki/Reich_Chancellery" title="Reich Chancellery">Reich Chancellery</a> with his foreign ministers and military chiefs that November, Hitler restated his intention of acquiring <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Lebensraum</i></span> for the German people. He ordered preparations for war in the East, to begin as early as 1938 and no later than 1943. In the event of his death, the conference minutes, recorded as the <a href="/wiki/Hossbach_Memorandum" title="Hossbach Memorandum">Hossbach Memorandum</a>, were to be regarded as his "political testament".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAigner1985264_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAigner1985264-218">[214]</a></sup> He felt that a severe decline in living standards in Germany as a result of the economic crisis could only be stopped by military aggression aimed at seizing Austria and <a href="/wiki/Czechoslovakia" title="Czechoslovakia">Czechoslovakia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMesserschmidt1990636–637_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMesserschmidt1990636–637-219">[215]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarr197273–78_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarr197273–78-220">[216]</a></sup> Hitler urged quick action before Britain and France gained a permanent lead in the <a href="/wiki/Arms_race" title="Arms race">arms race</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMesserschmidt1990636–637_219-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMesserschmidt1990636–637-219">[215]</a></sup> In early 1938, in the wake of the <a href="/wiki/Blomberg%E2%80%93Fritsch_affair" title="Blomberg–Fritsch affair">Blomberg–Fritsch Affair</a>, Hitler asserted control of the military-foreign policy apparatus, dismissing Neurath as foreign minister and appointing himself as War Minister.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery,_''Origins_of_WWII_Reconsidered''1999_214-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery,_''Origins_of_WWII_Reconsidered''1999-214">[210]</a></sup> From early 1938 onwards, Hitler was carrying out a foreign policy ultimately aimed at war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMesserschmidt1990638_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMesserschmidt1990638-221">[217]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_II">World War II</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Matsuoka_visits_Hitler.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="309" data-file-width="340" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Matsuoka_visits_Hitler.jpg/220px-Matsuoka_visits_Hitler.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Matsuoka_visits_Hitler.jpg/330px-Matsuoka_visits_Hitler.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/02/Matsuoka_visits_Hitler.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Matsuoka_visits_Hitler.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hitler and the Japanese foreign minister, <span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja-Latn" style="font-style: normal;"><a href="/wiki/Y%C5%8Dsuke_Matsuoka" title="Yōsuke Matsuoka">Yōsuke Matsuoka</a></span></span>, at a meeting in Berlin in March 1941. In the background is <a href="/wiki/Joachim_von_Ribbentrop" title="Joachim von Ribbentrop">Joachim von Ribbentrop</a>.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_diplomatic_successes">Early diplomatic successes</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Alliance_with_Japan">Alliance with Japan</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Germany%E2%80%93Japan_relations" title="Germany–Japan relations">Germany–Japan relations</a></div>
<p>In February 1938, on the advice of his newly appointed foreign minister, the strongly pro-Japanese <a href="/wiki/Joachim_von_Ribbentrop" title="Joachim von Ribbentrop">Joachim von Ribbentrop</a>, Hitler ended the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sino-German_cooperation_until_1941" title="Sino-German cooperation until 1941">Sino-German alliance</a> with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Republic_of_China_(1912%E2%80%9349)" title="Republic of China (1912–49)">Republic of China</a> to instead enter into an alliance with the more modern and powerful <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">Empire of Japan</a>. Hitler announced German recognition of <a href="/wiki/Manchukuo" title="Manchukuo">Manchukuo</a>, the Japanese-occupied state in <a href="/wiki/Manchuria" title="Manchuria">Manchuria</a>, and renounced German claims to their former colonies in the Pacific held by Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBloch1992178–179_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBloch1992178–179-222">[218]</a></sup> Hitler ordered an end to arms shipments to China and recalled all German officers working with the Chinese Army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBloch1992178–179_222-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBloch1992178–179-222">[218]</a></sup> In retaliation, Chinese General <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Latn" style="font-style: normal;"><a href="/wiki/Chiang_Kai-shek" title="Chiang Kai-shek">Chiang Kai-shek</a></span></span> cancelled all Sino-German economic agreements, depriving the Germans of many Chinese raw materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPlating201121_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPlating201121-223">[219]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Austria_and_Czechoslovakia">Austria and Czechoslovakia</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_137-004055,_Eger,_Besuch_Adolf_Hitlers.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="535" decoding="async" height="344" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Bundesarchiv_Bild_137-004055%2C_Eger%2C_Besuch_Adolf_Hitlers.jpg/230px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_137-004055%2C_Eger%2C_Besuch_Adolf_Hitlers.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Bundesarchiv_Bild_137-004055%2C_Eger%2C_Besuch_Adolf_Hitlers.jpg/345px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_137-004055%2C_Eger%2C_Besuch_Adolf_Hitlers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Bundesarchiv_Bild_137-004055%2C_Eger%2C_Besuch_Adolf_Hitlers.jpg/460px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_137-004055%2C_Eger%2C_Besuch_Adolf_Hitlers.jpg 2x" width="230"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_137-004055,_Eger,_Besuch_Adolf_Hitlers.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>October 1938: Hitler is driven through the crowd in <a href="/wiki/Cheb" title="Cheb">Cheb</a> (German: <i lang="de">Eger</i>), in the <a href="/wiki/Sudetenland" title="Sudetenland">Sudetenland</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>On 12 March 1938, Hitler announced the unification of Austria with <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> in the <i><a href="/wiki/Anschluss" title="Anschluss">Anschluss</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButlerYoung1989159_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButlerYoung1989159-224">[220]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBullock1962434_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBullock1962434-225">[221]</a></sup> Hitler then turned his attention to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethnic_German" title="Ethnic German">ethnic German</a> population of the <a href="/wiki/Sudetenland" title="Sudetenland">Sudetenland</a> region of Czechoslovakia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery2005425_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery2005425-226">[222]</a></sup> On 28–29 March 1938, Hitler held a series of secret meetings in Berlin with <a href="/wiki/Konrad_Henlein" title="Konrad Henlein">Konrad Henlein</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Sudeten_German_Party" title="Sudeten German Party">Sudeten German Party</a>, the largest of the ethnic German parties of the Sudetenland. The men agreed that Henlein would demand increased autonomy for <a href="/wiki/Sudeten_Germans" title="Sudeten Germans">Sudeten Germans</a> from the Czechoslovakian government, thus providing a pretext for German military action against Czechoslovakia. In April 1938 Henlein told the <a href="/wiki/Minister_of_Foreign_Affairs_(Hungary)" title="Minister of Foreign Affairs (Hungary)">foreign minister</a> of <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a> that "whatever the Czech government might offer, he would always raise still higher demands ... he wanted to sabotage an understanding by any means because this was the only method to blow up Czechoslovakia quickly".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinberg1980334–335_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinberg1980334–335-227">[223]</a></sup> In private, Hitler considered the Sudeten issue unimportant; his real intention was a war of conquest against Czechoslovakia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinberg1980338–340_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinberg1980338–340-228">[224]</a></sup>
</p><p>In April Hitler ordered the OKW to prepare for
<span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Fall_Gr%C3%BCn_(Czechoslovakia)" title="Fall Grün (Czechoslovakia)">Fall Grün</a></i></span> (Case Green), the code name for an invasion of Czechoslovakia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinberg1980366_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinberg1980366-229">[225]</a></sup> As a result of intense French and British diplomatic pressure, on 5 September Czechoslovakian President <a href="/wiki/Edvard_Bene%C5%A1" title="Edvard Beneš">Edvard Beneš</a> unveiled the "Fourth Plan" for constitutional reorganisation of his country, which agreed to most of Henlein's demands for Sudeten autonomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinberg1980418–419_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinberg1980418–419-230">[226]</a></sup> Henlein's party responded to Beneš' offer by instigating a series of violent clashes with the Czechoslovakian police that led to the declaration of martial law in certain Sudeten districts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKee1988149–150_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKee1988149–150-231">[227]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinberg1980419_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinberg1980419-232">[228]</a></sup>
</p><p>Germany was dependent on imported oil; a confrontation with Britain over the Czechoslovakian dispute could curtail Germany's oil supplies. This forced Hitler to call off <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Fall Grün</i></span>, originally planned for 1 October 1938.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray1984256–260_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray1984256–260-233">[229]</a></sup> On 29 September Hitler, <a href="/wiki/Neville_Chamberlain" title="Neville Chamberlain">Neville Chamberlain</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C3%89douard_Daladier" title="Édouard Daladier">Édouard Daladier</a>, and Mussolini attended a one-day conference in Munich that led to the <a href="/wiki/Munich_Agreement" title="Munich Agreement">Munich Agreement</a>, which handed over the Sudetenland districts to Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBullock1962469_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBullock1962469-234">[230]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOvery,_''The_Munich_Crisis''1999207_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOvery,_''The_Munich_Crisis''1999207-235">[231]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chamberlain was satisfied with the Munich conference, calling the outcome "<a href="/wiki/Peace_for_our_time" title="Peace for our time">peace for our time</a>", while Hitler was angered about the missed opportunity for war in 1938;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKee1988202–203_236-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKee1988202–203-236">[232]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinberg1980462–463_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinberg1980462–463-237">[233]</a></sup> he expressed his disappointment in a speech on 9 October in <a href="/wiki/Saarbr%C3%BCcken" title="Saarbrücken">Saarbrücken</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMesserschmidt1990672_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMesserschmidt1990672-238">[234]</a></sup> In Hitler's view, the British-brokered peace, although favourable to the ostensible German demands, was a diplomatic defeat which spurred his intent of limiting British power to pave the way for the eastern expansion of Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMesserschmidt1990671,_682–683_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMesserschmidt1990671,_682–683-239">[235]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERothwell200190–91_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERothwell200190–91-240">[236]</a></sup> As a result of the summit, Hitler was selected <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> magazine's <a href="/wiki/Time_Person_of_the_Year" title="Time Person of the Year">Man of the Year</a> for 1938.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE''Time'',_January_1939_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE''Time'',_January_1939-241">[237]</a></sup>
</p><p>In late 1938 and early 1939, the continuing economic crisis caused by rearmament forced Hitler to make major defence cuts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray1984268_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray1984268-242">[238]</a></sup> In his "Export or die" <a href="/wiki/30_January_1939_Reichstag_speech" title="30 January 1939 Reichstag speech">speech of 30 January 1939</a>, he called for an economic offensive to increase German foreign exchange holdings to pay for raw materials such as high-grade iron needed for military weapons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray1984268_242-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray1984268-242">[238]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 14 March 1939, under threat from Hungary, <a href="/wiki/Slovak_Republic_(1939%E2%80%931945)" title="Slovak Republic (1939–1945)">Slovakia declared independence</a> and received protection from Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2005682_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2005682-243">[239]</a></sup> The next day, in violation of the Munich accord and possibly as a result of the deepening economic crisis requiring additional assets,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMurray1984268–269_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMurray1984268–269-244">[240]</a></sup> Hitler ordered the Wehrmacht to <a href="/wiki/Occupation_of_Czechoslovakia_(1938%E2%80%931945)" title="Occupation of Czechoslovakia (1938–1945)">invade the Czech rump state</a>, and from <a href="/wiki/Prague_Castle" title="Prague Castle">Prague Castle</a> he proclaimed the territory a <a href="/wiki/Protectorate_of_Bohemia_and_Moravia" title="Protectorate of Bohemia and Moravia">German protectorate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShirer1960448_245-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShirer1960448-245">[241]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Start_of_World_War_II">Start of World War II</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Causes_of_World_War_II" title="Causes of World War II">Causes of World War II</a></div>
<p>In private discussions in 1939, Hitler declared Britain the main enemy to be defeated and that Poland's obliteration was a necessary prelude for that goal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinberg1980562_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinberg1980562-246">[242]</a></sup> The eastern flank would be secured and land would be added to Germany's <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Lebensraum</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinberg1980579–581_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinberg1980579–581-247">[243]</a></sup> Offended by the British "guarantee" on 31 March 1939 of Polish independence, he said, "I shall brew them a devil's drink".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaiolo1998178_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaiolo1998178-248">[244]</a></sup> In a speech in <a href="/wiki/Wilhelmshaven" title="Wilhelmshaven">Wilhelmshaven</a> for the launch of the battleship <a href="/wiki/German_battleship_Tirpitz" title="German battleship Tirpitz"><i>Tirpitz</i></a> on 1 April, he threatened to denounce the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-German_Naval_Agreement" title="Anglo-German Naval Agreement">Anglo-German Naval Agreement</a> if the British continued to guarantee Polish independence, which he perceived as an "encirclement" policy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaiolo1998178_248-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaiolo1998178-248">[244]</a></sup> Poland was to either become a German satellite state or it would be neutralised in order to secure the Reich's eastern flank and prevent a possible British blockade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMesserschmidt1990688–690_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMesserschmidt1990688–690-249">[245]</a></sup> Hitler initially favoured the idea of a satellite state, but upon its rejection by the Polish government, he decided to invade and made this the main foreign policy goal of 1939.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinberg1980537–539,_557–560_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinberg1980537–539,_557–560-250">[246]</a></sup> On 3 April, Hitler ordered the military to prepare for <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Fall_Weiss_(1939)" title="Fall Weiss (1939)">Fall Weiss</a></i></span> ("Case White"), the plan for invading Poland on 25 August.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinberg1980537–539,_557–560_250-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinberg1980537–539,_557–560-250">[246]</a></sup> In a Reichstag speech on 28 April, he renounced both the Anglo-German Naval Agreement and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German%E2%80%93Polish_Non-Aggression_Pact" title="German–Polish Non-Aggression Pact">German–Polish Non-Aggression Pact</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinberg1980558_251-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinberg1980558-251">[247]</a></sup> Historians such as <a href="/wiki/William_Carr_(historian)" title="William Carr (historian)">William Carr</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gerhard_Weinberg" title="Gerhard Weinberg">Gerhard Weinberg</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ian_Kershaw" title="Ian Kershaw">Ian Kershaw</a> have argued that one reason for Hitler's rush to war was his fear of an early death. He had repeatedly claimed that he must lead Germany into war before he got too old, as his successors might lack his strength of will.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarr197276–77_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarr197276–77-252">[248]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw2000b36–37,_92_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw2000b36–37,_92-253">[249]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinberg2010792_254-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinberg2010792-254">[250]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hitler was concerned that a military attack against Poland could result in a premature war with Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMesserschmidt1990688–690_249-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMesserschmidt1990688–690-249">[245]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobertson1985212_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobertson1985212-255">[251]</a></sup> Hitler's foreign minister and former Ambassador to London, Joachim von Ribbentrop, assured him that neither Britain nor France would honour their commitments to Poland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBloch1992228_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBloch1992228-256">[252]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOveryWheatcroft198956_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOveryWheatcroft198956-257">[253]</a></sup> Accordingly, on 22 August 1939 Hitler ordered a military mobilisation against Poland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008497_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008497-258">[254]</a></sup>
</p><p>This plan required tacit Soviet support,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobertson1963181–187_259-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobertson1963181–187-259">[255]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Non-aggression_pact" title="Non-aggression pact">non-aggression pact</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Molotov%E2%80%93Ribbentrop_Pact" title="Molotov–Ribbentrop Pact">Molotov–Ribbentrop Pact</a>) between Germany and the Soviet Union, led by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Joseph Stalin</a>, included a secret agreement to partition Poland between the two countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEvans2005693_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEvans2005693-260">[256]</a></sup> Contrary to Ribbentrop's prediction that Britain would sever Anglo-Polish ties, Britain and Poland signed the Anglo-Polish alliance on 25 August 1939. This, along with news from Italy that Mussolini would not honour the <a href="/wiki/Pact_of_Steel" title="Pact of Steel">Pact of Steel</a>, prompted Hitler to postpone the attack on Poland from 25 August to 1 September.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBloch1992252–253_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBloch1992252–253-261">[257]</a></sup> Hitler unsuccessfully tried to manoeuvre the British into neutrality by offering them a non-aggression guarantee on 25 August; he then instructed Ribbentrop to present a last-minute peace plan with an impossibly short time limit in an effort to blame the imminent war on British and Polish inaction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinberg199585–94_262-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinberg199585–94-262">[258]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBloch1992255–257_263-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBloch1992255–257-263">[259]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 1 September 1939, Germany <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Poland" title="Invasion of Poland">invaded western Poland</a> under the pretext of having been denied claims to the <a href="/wiki/Free_City_of_Danzig" title="Free City of Danzig">Free City of Danzig</a> and the right to extraterritorial roads across the <a href="/wiki/Polish_Corridor" title="Polish Corridor">Polish Corridor</a>, which Germany had ceded under the Versailles Treaty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeinberg1980561–562,_583–584_264-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeinberg1980561–562,_583–584-264">[260]</a></sup> In response, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_and_French_declaration_of_war_on_Germany" title="British and French declaration of war on Germany">Britain and France declared war</a> on Germany on 3 September, surprising Hitler and prompting him to angrily ask Ribbentrop, "Now what?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBloch1992260_265-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBloch1992260-265">[261]</a></sup> France and Britain did not act on their declarations immediately, and on 17 September, Soviet forces invaded eastern Poland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHakim1995_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHakim1995-266">[262]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-S55480,_Polen,_Parade_vor_Adolf_Hitler.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="532" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-S55480%2C_Polen%2C_Parade_vor_Adolf_Hitler.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-S55480%2C_Polen%2C_Parade_vor_Adolf_Hitler.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-S55480%2C_Polen%2C_Parade_vor_Adolf_Hitler.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-S55480%2C_Polen%2C_Parade_vor_Adolf_Hitler.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-S55480%2C_Polen%2C_Parade_vor_Adolf_Hitler.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-S55480%2C_Polen%2C_Parade_vor_Adolf_Hitler.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-S55480,_Polen,_Parade_vor_Adolf_Hitler.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hitler reviews troops on the march during the <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Poland" title="Invasion of Poland">campaign against Poland</a> (September 1939).</div></div></div>
<p>The fall of Poland was followed by what contemporary journalists dubbed the "<a href="/wiki/Phoney_War" title="Phoney War">Phoney War</a>" or <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Sitzkrieg</i></span> ("sitting war"). Hitler instructed the two newly appointed <a href="/wiki/Gauleiter" title="Gauleiter">Gauleiters</a> of north-western Poland, <a href="/wiki/Albert_Forster" title="Albert Forster">Albert Forster</a> of <a href="/wiki/Reichsgau_Danzig-West_Prussia" title="Reichsgau Danzig-West Prussia">Reichsgau Danzig-West Prussia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Greiser" title="Arthur Greiser">Arthur Greiser</a> of <a href="/wiki/Reichsgau_Wartheland" title="Reichsgau Wartheland">Reichsgau Wartheland</a>, to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Germanise" title="Germanise">Germanise</a> their areas, with "no questions asked" about how this was accomplished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERees1997141–145_267-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERees1997141–145-267">[263]</a></sup> In Forster's area, ethnic Poles merely had to sign forms stating that they had German blood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008527_268-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008527-268">[264]</a></sup> In contrast, Greiser agreed with Himmler and carried out an <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_cleansing" title="Ethnic cleansing">ethnic cleansing</a> campaign towards Poles. Greiser soon complained that Forster was allowing thousands of Poles to be accepted as "racial" Germans and thus endangered German "racial purity".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERees1997141–145_267-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERees1997141–145-267">[263]</a></sup> Hitler refrained from getting involved. This inaction has been advanced as an example of the theory of "working towards the Führer", in which Hitler issued vague instructions and expected his subordinates to work out policies on their own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERees1997141–145_267-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERees1997141–145-267">[263]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWelch200188–89_269-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWelch200188–89-269">[265]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another dispute pitched one side represented by <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Himmler" title="Heinrich Himmler">Heinrich Himmler</a> and Greiser, who championed ethnic cleansing in Poland, against another represented by Göring and Hans Frank (<a href="/wiki/General_Government" title="General Government">governor-general</a> of occupied Poland), who called for turning Poland into the "granary" of the Reich. On 12 February 1940, the dispute was initially settled in favour of the Göring–Frank view, which ended the economically disruptive mass expulsions. On 15 May 1940, Himmler issued a memo entitled "Some Thoughts on the Treatment of Alien Population in the East", calling for the expulsion of the entire Jewish population of Europe into Africa and the reduction of the Polish population to a "leaderless class of labourers". Hitler called Himmler's memo "good and correct", and, ignoring Göring and Frank, implemented the Himmler–Greiser policy in Poland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERees1997148–149_270-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERees1997148–149-270">[266]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Adolf_Hitler_in_Paris_1940.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2930" data-file-width="2019" decoding="async" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/db/Adolf_Hitler_in_Paris_1940.jpg/170px-Adolf_Hitler_in_Paris_1940.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/db/Adolf_Hitler_in_Paris_1940.jpg/255px-Adolf_Hitler_in_Paris_1940.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/db/Adolf_Hitler_in_Paris_1940.jpg/340px-Adolf_Hitler_in_Paris_1940.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Adolf_Hitler_in_Paris_1940.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hitler visits Paris with architect <a href="/wiki/Albert_Speer" title="Albert Speer">Albert Speer</a> (left) and sculptor <a href="/wiki/Arno_Breker" title="Arno Breker">Arno Breker</a> (right), 23 June 1940.</div></div></div>
<p>On 9 April, German forces <a href="/wiki/Operation_Weser%C3%BCbung" title="Operation Weserübung">invaded Denmark and Norway</a>. On the same day Hitler proclaimed the birth of the <a href="/wiki/Greater_Germanic_Reich" title="Greater Germanic Reich">Greater Germanic Reich</a>, his vision of a united empire of Germanic nations of Europe in which the Dutch, Flemish, and Scandinavians were joined into a "racially pure" polity under German leadership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWinkler200774_271-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWinkler200774-271">[267]</a></sup> In May 1940, Germany <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_France" title="Battle of France">attacked France</a>, and conquered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_occupation_of_Luxembourg_in_World_War_II" title="German occupation of Luxembourg in World War II">Luxembourg</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Netherlands" title="Battle of the Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Belgium" title="Battle of Belgium">Belgium</a>. These victories prompted Mussolini to have Italy join forces with Hitler on 10 June. France and Germany signed an <a href="/wiki/Armistice_of_22_June_1940" title="Armistice of 22 June 1940">armistice</a> on 22 June.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShirer1960696–730_272-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShirer1960696–730-272">[268]</a></sup> Kershaw notes that Hitler's popularity within Germany – and German support for the war – reached its peak when he returned to Berlin on 6 July from his tour of Paris.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008562_273-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008562-273">[269]</a></sup> Following the unexpected swift victory, Hitler promoted twelve generals to the rank of <a href="/wiki/Field_marshal" title="Field marshal">field marshal</a> during the <a href="/wiki/1940_Field_Marshal_Ceremony" title="1940 Field Marshal Ceremony">1940 Field Marshal Ceremony</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeighton20087–9_274-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeighton20087–9-274">[270]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis199394_275-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis199394-275">[271]</a></sup>
</p><p>Britain, whose troops were forced to evacuate France by sea from <a href="/wiki/Dunkirk_evacuation" title="Dunkirk evacuation">Dunkirk</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShirer1960731–737_276-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShirer1960731–737-276">[272]</a></sup> continued to fight alongside <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">other British dominions</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Atlantic" title="Battle of the Atlantic">Battle of the Atlantic</a>. Hitler made peace overtures to the new British leader, <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a>, and upon their rejection he ordered a series of aerial attacks on <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">Royal Air Force</a> airbases and radar stations in southeast England. On 7 September the systematic nightly bombing of London began. The German Luftwaffe failed to defeat the Royal Air Force in what became known as the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Britain" title="Battle of Britain">Battle of Britain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShirer1960774–782_277-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShirer1960774–782-277">[273]</a></sup> By the end of September, Hitler realised that air superiority for the invasion of Britain (in <a href="/wiki/Operation_Sea_Lion" title="Operation Sea Lion">Operation Sea Lion</a>) could not be achieved, and ordered the operation postponed. The <a href="/wiki/The_Blitz" title="The Blitz">nightly air raids</a> on British cities intensified and continued for months, including London, <a href="/wiki/Plymouth" title="Plymouth">Plymouth</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Coventry" title="Coventry">Coventry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008563,_569,_570_278-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008563,_569,_570-278">[274]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 27 September 1940, the <a href="/wiki/Tripartite_Pact" title="Tripartite Pact">Tripartite Pact</a> was signed in Berlin by <a href="/wiki/Sabur%C5%8D_Kurusu" title="Saburō Kurusu">Saburō Kurusu</a> of <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">Imperial Japan</a>, Hitler, and Italian foreign minister Ciano,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008580_279-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008580-279">[275]</a></sup> and later expanded to include Hungary, Romania, and <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>, thus yielding the <a href="/wiki/Axis_powers" title="Axis powers">Axis powers</a>. Hitler's attempt to integrate the Soviet Union into the anti-British bloc failed after inconclusive talks between Hitler and <a href="/wiki/Vyacheslav_Molotov" title="Vyacheslav Molotov">Molotov</a> in Berlin in November, and he ordered preparations for the invasion of the Soviet Union.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoberts200658–60_280-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoberts200658–60-280">[276]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greater_Germanic_Reich.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="785" data-file-width="950" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Greater_Germanic_Reich.png/170px-Greater_Germanic_Reich.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Greater_Germanic_Reich.png/255px-Greater_Germanic_Reich.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Greater_Germanic_Reich.png/340px-Greater_Germanic_Reich.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Greater_Germanic_Reich.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Boundaries of the Nazi planned <a href="/wiki/Greater_Germanic_Reich" title="Greater Germanic Reich">Greater Germanic Reich</a></div></div></div>
<p>In early 1941, German forces were deployed to North Africa, the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a>, and the Middle East. In February, <a href="/wiki/Operation_Sonnenblume" title="Operation Sonnenblume">German forces arrived in Libya</a> to bolster the Italian presence. In April, Hitler launched the <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Yugoslavia" title="Invasion of Yugoslavia">invasion of Yugoslavia</a>, quickly followed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Greece" title="Battle of Greece">invasion of Greece</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008604–605_281-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKershaw2008604–605-281">[277]</a></sup> In May, German forces were sent to support <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Iraqi_War" title="Anglo-Iraqi War">Iraqi forces fighting against the British</a> and to <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Crete" title="Battle of Crete">invade Crete</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKurowski2005141–142_282-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKurowski2005141–142-282">[278]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Path_to_defeat">Path to defeat</span></h3>
<p>On 22 June 1941, contravening the <a href="/wiki/Molotov%E2%80%93Ribbentrop_Pact" title="Molotov–Ribbentrop Pact">Molotov–Ribbentrop Pact</a> of 1939, over three million Axis troops attacked <a href="/wiki/Operation_Barbarossa" title="Operation Barbarossa">the Soviet Union</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMineau20041_283-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMineau20041-283">[279]</a></sup> This offensive (codenamed <a href="/wiki/Operation_Barbarossa" title="Operation Barbarossa">Operation Barbarossa</a>) was intended to destroy the Soviet Union and seize its natural resources for subsequent aggression against the Western powers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGlantz20019_284-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGlantz20019-284">[280]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKoch1988_285-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKoch1988-285">[281]</a></sup> The invasion conquered a huge area, including the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_region" title="Baltic region">Baltic</a> republics, <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a>, and West <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>. By early August, Axis troops had advanced 500 km (310 mi) and won the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Smolensk_(1941)" title="Battle of Smolensk (1941)">Battle of Smolensk</a>.
